This is pure research — do NOT write any code.\n\nContext: We have an app called ANT (A Nice Terminal) that currently has an MCP server (packages/mcp) that wraps REST API endpoints as MCP tools for AI agents (Claude Code, Cursor, etc.) to interact with terminal sessions. The current flow is: AI agent → MCP server (stdio) → REST API → Express server → node-pty. This is convoluted. We want to design a much more direct, robust CLI.\n\nResearch these questions thoroughly:\n\n1. **CLI architecture for terminal management**:\n   - How do tmux, zellij, screen implement their CLI interfaces for session management?\n   - The client-server model: tmux's approach (Unix domain socket), zellij's approach\n   - How does the `gh` CLI achieve such a smooth developer experience?\n   - Modern CLI frameworks: oclif, commander, yargs, clap (Rust), cobra (Go) — which is best for a tool that needs real-time terminal interaction?\n   - How do tools like lazygit, lazydocker achieve their smooth TUI + CLI hybrid interface?\n\n2. **Direct terminal interaction without MCP overhead**:\n   - Unix domain sockets vs. named pipes vs. gRPC vs. REST for local IPC\n   - How could a CLI tool directly attach to / control managed terminal sessions?\n   - The approach of tools like dtach, abduco, reptyr for session attachment\n   - How Claude Code itself manages its terminal interactions\n   - How to design a CLI that feels native but connects to a session manager daemon\n\n3. **Modern patterns for AI-agent ↔ terminal integration**:\n   - How are AI coding assistants (Claude Code, Cursor, Windsurf, Aider, Continue) handling terminal interaction in 2025-2026?\n   - What protocols/standards are emerging for AI-terminal communication?\n   - Is MCP still the right protocol, or are there better alternatives emerging?\n   - How to make the CLI both human-usable AND agent-usable without separate codepaths\n   - The \"tool use\" pattern vs. direct shell integration\n\n4. **Session daemon architecture**:\n   - Best practices for a long-running daemon that manages terminal sessions\n   - How to handle daemon lifecycle (start, stop, crash recovery, auto-start)\n   - State persistence and recovery after daemon restart\n   - Multi-user / multi-agent concurrent access patterns\n   - How to expose sessions over network (for remote access) while keeping local access fast\n\n5. **Specific technical approaches worth considering**:\n   - Writing the core in Rust with Node.js/TypeScript bindings (napi-rs pattern)\n   - Using SQLite as the communication layer (like Litestream / Turso patterns)\n   - gRPC with bidirectional streaming for real-time terminal I/O\n   - This architecture enables multiple clients to attach to a single session, supports detached sessions, and allows for remote access capabilities.\n\n## Communication Protocol\n\nClientToServerMsg encompasses key events, terminal resize notifications, action dispatches (new pane, close tab, switch mode), and session attach/detach commands, while ServerToClientMsg carries rendered terminal output, session switch directives, exit signals, and pane render updates for subscribed clients. Communication uses Protocol Buffers (protobuf) for efficient binary serialization.\n\n## Server Architecture\n\nThe server is the brain of Zellij. When a session starts, it spawns several dedicated threads with well-defined responsibilities. All threads communicate through a typed Thread Bus, which wraps MPSC channel senders into a single ThreadSenders struct. This pattern ensures that any thread can reach any other thread without circular dependencies, while keeping the message types strongly typed through Rust enums like ScreenInstruction, PtyInstruction, and PluginInstruction.\n\n## Plugin System\n\nPlugins are compiled to WebAssembly and executed in a wasmi runtime sandbox. This means plugins can extend or replace Zellij's UI elements without risking the stability of the core application.\n\n## Recent Features (2025)\n\nNative Windows support was the headline addition in Zellij 0.44.0, released on March 23. Here's what the search results reveal:\n\n## Performance Comparison Overview\n\nBoth Unix domain socket and Named pipe offer better performance than TCP/IP because they can avoid the networking overhead.\n\n### Block Size Dependencies\n\nPerformance varies significantly based on message size. The performance of each method depends on the block size, with pipes being slightly faster than sockets when using the smallest block sizes of 100 bytes and 500 bytes, however, when using the biggest block sizes of 10 Kbytes and 1 Mbyte, sockets were faster than pipes.\n\nSpecifically, with a 100-byte block size the fastest IPC method was the named pipe at 318 Mbits/s, while the UNIX socket transmitted at 245 Mbits/s, making named pipes approximately 30% faster than UNIX sockets with a block size of 100 bytes.\n\n### gRPC vs Raw IPC\n\ngRPC is about a factor of 10 slower than blocking I/O over Unix domain sockets. However, the ~100 µs unary-call latency overhead of gRPC for local IPC is entirely acceptable for purposes where the overhead is outweighed by the benefits of using only a single library for IPC and RPC, and having well-defined, strongly-typed interfaces.\n\n### Practical Recommendations\n\nUnix Domain Sockets are dominant in all scenarios—lower latency (30µs avg RTT) and higher throughput (210MB/s) than FIFOs, and should be used for most local IPC.\n\nFor .NET Core IPC, Named Pipes should be used for local, high-performance communication on the same machine with simple data, while gRPC should be used for cross-machine, scalable communication with remote services and complex data.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"3806796d-0b7b-439b-9e5a-ecbfc58ecdb6","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:06.366Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"b5f1600e-f6e5-4f7e-854f-3093e4f481e4","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

However if a given session name represents either a relative or absolute path it is used unmodified.\n\nThe system uses unix(7) domain sockets representing a session, which can be deleted and recreated by sending SIGUSR1 to the server process.\n\n## Core Functionality\n\n**Session Creation and Attachment:**\nAbduco provides session management i.e. it allows programs to be run independently from its controlling terminal. That is programs can be detached - run in the background - and then later reattached.\n\n**Detaching:**\nCTRL-\\ detaches from the active session. This detach key can be changed by means of the -e command line option, -e ^q would for example set it to CTRL-q.\n\n**Session Status:**\nThe session exit status of the command being run is always kept and reported either upon command termination or on reconnection.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"78e86e04-462b-45b5-bc53-8a2942f8ec90","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:17.496Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"c1103917-79c7-436c-aacc-4bf517f7bba2","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Bubble Tea programs are comprised of a model that describes the application state and three simple methods on that model: Init, a function that returns an initial command for the application to run, Update, a function that handles incoming events and updates the model accordingly, and View, a function that renders the UI based on the data in the model.\n\n## Lazygit and Lazydocker Examples\n\nLazydocker's goal is to make information about Docker containers available in one terminal window with every common command living one keypress away. These tools demonstrate the practical application of TUI design patterns for interactive command-line interfaces.\n\n## Hybrid Architecture in Practice\n\nThe Bubble Tea framework can be used for a non-TUI application, or to provide an additional non-TUI mode to your Bubble Tea programs. For example, your program could behave like a daemon if output is not a TTY.\n\n## Real-World Implementations\n\nSeveral projects follow this pattern. The primary way to configure a service in systemd for automatic restart is by modifying its unit file. It's generally recommended to create an override file to avoid issues during package updates.\n\n### Crash Recovery Strategies\n\nSystemd provides different restart policies: Restart=always – The service always restarts, even if it was manually stopped. Restart=on-failure – Restarts only if the service exits with an error (but not if stopped manually). Restart=on-abnormal – Restarts the service if it crashes due to a signal (like a segmentation fault). Restart=on-watchdog – Restart the service if it times out while running.\n\n### Critical Configuration Directives\n\nThe two important directives in a Systemd unit file are Restart and RestartSec. They control the crash behavior of the service. Restart specifies when the service should restart, and RestartSec specifies how long it should wait before restarting after a crash.\n\nSystemd also has StartLimitIntervalSec and StartLimitBurst directives within the [Unit] section to prevent a failing service from restarting too many times in a short period, potentially overloading the system. This means if the service fails 5 times within 500 seconds, systemd will stop attempting to restart it.\n\n### 2026 Best Practices\n\nRun daemon-reload after every edit. Forgetting this is the number one cause of \"my changes aren't working.\" Use drop-in overrides instead of editing vendor unit files. Run systemctl edit myservice to create override files that survive package updates.\n\nSet appropriate Restart policy. Use Restart=on-failure for most services. Use Restart=always only for critical services that must never be down.\n\n## launchd (macOS)\n\nLike the inetd process, launchd launches daemons on-demand. Daemons launched in this manner can be shut down during periods of inactivity and relaunched as needed. When a service request comes in, if the daemon is not running, launchd automatically starts the daemon to process the request.\n\nThese practices ensure reliable daemon lifecycle management with minimal manual intervention.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"f5eb3e8c-ae32-4301-9e4a-1b3d4589c6fb","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:21.250Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"905db318-5181-4bf9-956c-f4988996f0b3","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Ben Johnson's tool continuously replicates SQLite databases to S3-compatible storage by streaming WAL changes in near-real-time.\n\n**Turso**: Turso Database is an in-process SQL database written in Rust, compatible with SQLite. An embedded replica is a local SQLite file that maintains synchronization with your primary database hosted on Turso Cloud — enabling zero-latency network reads, with queries executing directly against the local SQLite file and write operations forwarding to the primary database.\n\n**2025-2026 Pattern**: A constellation of technologies — Turso, Cloudflare D1, LibSQL, Litestream, and the embedded database movement — converged to solve SQLite's real limitations while preserving its absurd simplicity.\n\nThe practical pattern for IPC seems to involve using SQLite as a shared local data store accessed by multiple processes, with Litestream handling replication and Turso providing cloud synchronization for distributed scenarios.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"537a7f99-d7c2-4753-837f-ad9c0d91a387","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:46.469Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"3d390013-777f-44e9-ae8a-8fcf4310f446","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

The /dev directory contains special device files that represent various hardware and kernel resources. This principle extends to terminal management through pseudo-terminal devices.\n\n## /dev/pts: Pseudo-Terminal Architecture\n\n/dev/pts is the virtual filesystem where pseudo-terminal devices reside. /dev/ptmx is the \"pseudo-terminal master multiplexer\". Opening it returns a file descriptor of a master node and causes an associated slave node /dev/pts/N to be created.\n\nPseudo terminals appear dynamically when users open terminal windows or SSH sessions. Each terminal session gets a separate pseudo-terminal device, allowing concurrent independent shells.\n\n## Session Manager Daemon Implementation\n\nMultiplexer applications like screen and tmux serve as virtual terminals, decoupling text mode applications from the actual physical terminal. They enable running multiple terminal sessions within a single window, facilitating efficient multitasking.\n\nOn the first start, tmux starts a server with a set of ptmx corresponding to its panes. Clients of tmux use a default unix socket to find and connect to the server.\n\n## Daemon Architecture\n\nA daemon runs in the background without a controlling terminal. This guarantees that the process never gets terminal-related signals from the kernel: SIGINT, SIGTSTP, and SIGHUP.\n\nPseudoterminals allow programs (terminal emulators, remote-login daemons, multiplexers) to create many independent terminal sessions without dedicated hardware. Master/slave separation enforces a clean I/O boundary: the slave behaves like a tty device for interactive programs; the master can be controlled by another process, subject to permissions.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"fe2e5c42-ea20-431b-84dd-35561de47afc","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:45:48.798Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"42ff5d19-ba3e-4743-ac5a-302bd67ed1bf","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

In one documented deployment, three MCP servers consumed 143,000 of 200,000 tokens—72% of the available context—leaving only 57,000 tokens for actual conversation, retrieved documents, and reasoning.\n\n**Serialization Performance:**\nStandard MCP implementations add network and serialization overhead that becomes problematic for high-frequency applications.\n\n**Scalability Challenges:**\nThe protocol relies on long-lived, \"stateful\" sessions, which can make it harder to deploy MCP servers across multiple instances or behind load balancers, since the session state is typically stored on the server handling the connection, rather than shared across machines.\n\n**Production Deployment Issues:**\nEnterprises are deploying MCP and running into a predictable set of problems: audit trails, SSO-integrated auth, gateway behavior, and configuration portability.\n\n## Alternatives and Solutions\n\n**Cloudflare's Code Mode:**\nCloudflare developed \"Code Mode,\" a technique where agents write code against typed SDKs instead of calling individual tools. Scalable session handling is planned to define how sessions are created, resumed, and migrated so that server restarts and scale-out events are transparent to connected clients.\n\n## Stateless Architecture Changes\n\nThe vision is that agentic applications are stateful, but the protocol itself doesn't need to be—a stateless protocol enables scale, while still providing features to support stateful application sessions when needed. As part of this roadmap, the project will standardize what \"stateless\" means across all official SDKs to ensure consistent behavior.\n\n## Implementation Timeline\n\nThe goal is to finalize the required Spec Enhancement Proposals (SEPs) in the first quarter of 2026 for inclusion in the next specification release, tentatively slated for June of 2026.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"480297b9-6035-4c9c-b194-ba5c895e2069","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:46:02.304Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6a0ef943-6aa1-451b-9510-08e7bc39650a","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here's what I found:\n\n## Tmux Client-Server Architecture with Unix Domain Socket Implementation\n\nTmux operates on a client-server architecture where all communication flows through Unix socket files stored in a temporary directory.\n\n### How It Works\n\nTmux is a client-server implementation where when we start tmux, we are essentially connected to a server instance via a socket connection. The codebase is written in C and implements a client-server architecture using Unix sockets for inter-process communication.\n\nMore specifically, clients of tmux (tmux attach) use a default unix socket to find and connect to the server, located at paths like /tmp/tmux-1000/default.\n\n### Server Architecture\n\nThe server manages a hierarchical structure of sessions, windows, and panes, each with their own terminal state and rendering. Written in Rust, Zellij is a next-generation terminal multiplexer that combines performance, sane defaults, and discoverability.\n\n## Client-Server Architecture\n\nKeeping sessions alive in the background involves a client/server architecture. The client runs in the user's terminal like any other program and communicates over IPC with a server holding the state of the terminal session (open programs, pane and tab layout, etc.)\n\n## Server Architecture\n\nThe server is the brain of Zellij. When a session starts, it spawns several dedicated threads, each with a well-defined responsibility. All threads communicate through a typed Thread Bus (thread_bus.rs), which wraps MPSC channel senders into a single ThreadSenders struct. This pattern ensures that any thread can reach any other thread without circular dependencies, while keeping the message types strongly typed through Rust enums like ScreenInstruction, PtyInstruction, and PluginInstruction.\n\n## Communication Protocol\n\nClientToServerMsg encompasses key events, terminal resize notifications, action dispatches (new pane, close tab, switch mode), and session attach/detach commands. ServerToClientMsg carries rendered terminal output, session switch directives, exit signals, and pane render updates for subscribed clients. This binary protocol is defined in the client_server_contract Protobuf schema within zellij-utils/, ensuring forward-compatible serialization across client and server versions.\n\n## Plugin System\n\nZellij's plugin system is one of its most distinctive features. Plugins are compiled to WebAssembly and executed in a wasmi runtime sandbox. This means plugins can extend or replace Zellij's UI elements (status bar, tab bar, pane decorations) without risking the stability of the core application.\n\n## Web Client\n\nIn the recent Zellij version we released a built-in web client, allowing users to attach to these sessions from the browser - essentially making a dedicated terminal application optional. Since Zellij is written in Rust, we naturally decided to use Rust to develop the web-server as well. We chose axum as our webserver because we liked its mix-and-match approach. It integrates seamlessly with tokio (our async runtime) and that it provides a \"native\" websocket implementation (using tokio-tungstenite under the hood).\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"55d0d576-cb09-4b9c-b80f-9c126a6dcaa7","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:23.509Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"d48c8d79-b9cd-4ba4-888d-a4f564e95dcd","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

However, the ~100 µs unary-call latency overhead of gRPC for local IPC is entirely acceptable for purposes where the overhead is outweighed by the benefits of using only a single library for IPC and RPC with well-defined, strongly-typed interfaces.\n\nRecent benchmarks from 2025 show practical results: Swapping gRPC's TCP layer for UDS cut latency by ~20% in an order-processing system.\n\n### Technology Characteristics\n\nUnix domain sockets (UDS) is a widely supported IPC technology that is the best choice for building cross-platform apps, usable on Linux, macOS, and Windows 10/Windows Server 2019 or later. Here's a summary of the findings:\n\n## Key Findings\n\n### Systemd (Linux)\n\nSystemd is the most widely adopted init system in modern Linux distributions, offering powerful and flexible service management capabilities, including automatic restarts.\n\n**Restart Policies:**\nSystemd provides different restart policies: Restart=always (service always restarts), Restart=on-failure (restarts only if exits with error), Restart=on-abnormal (restarts if it crashes due to a signal), and Restart=on-watchdog (restart if it times out).\n\n**Best Practices:**\nRun daemon-reload after every edit, use drop-in overrides instead of editing vendor unit files, and run systemctl edit to create override files that survive package updates. Use Restart=on-failure for most services and Restart=always only for critical services that must never be down.\n\n**Crash Recovery Controls:**\nSystemd has StartLimitIntervalSec and StartLimitBurst directives to prevent a failing service from restarting too many times in a short period—for example, if a service fails 5 times within 500 seconds, systemd will stop attempting to restart it.\n\n### Launchd (macOS)\n\nThe root process on OS X is launchd, which initializes the system and coordinates the launching of system daemons in an orderly manner. Like inetd, launchd launches daemons on-demand, can shut them down during periods of inactivity, and automatically starts a daemon when a service request comes in.\n\nThe search results provided current information with the most recent source from February 2026 covering comprehensive systemd best practices for 2026.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"ae5a717e-8a05-4f33-9e99-ba84b4c60445","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:47:47.532Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"8da0a687-1bb5-495f-be45-5a08ac065128","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

Here's what the search results reveal about why CLI tools are gaining traction over MCP for AI agents in 2026:\n\n## Key Arguments for CLI Over MCP\n\n**Context Efficiency**\nMCP is a context hog—a typical MCP server doesn't just expose needed tools but dumps an entire schema into the agent's context window including tool definitions, parameter descriptions, authentication flows, and state management. For example, a GitHub MCP server ships with 93 tools with a total context cost of around 55,000 tokens before you've even asked a single question.\n\nBy contrast, AI models have been trained on billions of lines of terminal interactions from Stack Overflow answers, GitHub repos, documentation, and tutorials, so they already understand CLI commands natively.\n\n**Reliability & Performance**\nIn benchmarks comparing 75 runs, CLI won on every efficiency metric—10 to 32× cheaper, and 100% reliable versus MCP's 72%. For a simple task like \"what language is this repo?\", the CLI agent needed 1,365 tokens while the MCP agent needed 44,026.\n\n**Debugging Simplicity**\nWith CLI, standard debugging tools work like curl and set -x, but MCP failures cascade across transport errors, JSON-RPC framing issues, and schema validation failures, with developers spending hours on connection issues.\n\n## When MCP Still Makes Sense\n\nHowever, CLI wins on efficiency, but MCP wins on governance with per-user OAuth, explicit tool boundaries, and structured audit trails—choosing depends on who the agent is acting for. Developer tools with polished CLIs represent only 5% of integrations; the other 95% are SaaS systems where no CLI exists.\n\n## The Emerging Consensus\n\nPractitioners are converging on making the transport decision per tool integration rather than per system, and the design quality of the tool interface determines whether the agent works well, not the protocol. Born as a community fork of the popular tui-rs crate in 2023, Ratatui carries forward the legacy while actively evolving to meet contemporary needs.\n\n## Architecture\n\n### Core Rendering Approach\n\nRatatui is based on the principle of immediate rendering with intermediate buffers, meaning that for each frame, your app must render all widgets that are supposed to be part of the UI. The immediate rendering approach combined with intermediate buffers means developers build their entire UI for each frame, providing maximum flexibility while minimizing the actual terminal output through intelligent buffering.\n\n### Modular Structure\n\nStarting with Ratatui 0.30.0, the project was reorganized into a modular workspace to improve compilation times, API stability, and dependency management. The ARCHITECTURE.md document explains the crate organization and modular workspace structure.\n\n### Key Architectural Features\n\nRatatui offers constraint-based responsive layouts that automatically adapt to any terminal size, allowing you to build complex nested layouts with horizontal and vertical splits, automatically sized containers, and percentage-based constraints.\n\nRatatui provides UI widgets for building a TUI, focusing on the UI aspects and leveraging other crates for the low level terminal interaction.\n\n## Best Practices\n\n### Performance\n\nRatatui maintains excellent performance characteristics: minimal ANSI escape sequences through intelligent diffing, efficient buffer management reducing terminal updates, and Rust's zero-cost abstractions ensuring no runtime overhead.\n\n### Application Structure\n\nRatatui is a lightweight, unopinionated toolkit that doesn't impose an Elm-style model or a specific app structure—you keep your own state, run your own event loop (typically with crossterm, termion, or termwiz), and call terminal.draw() to render.\n\n### Terminal Management\n\nIt's recommended to implement the Drop trait on your terminal-managing struct to ensure that if your app terminates unexpectedly, it calls the exit function and restores the terminal.\n\n### Getting Started\n\nRatatui has templates available to help you get started quickly using the cargo-generate command. For guidance on different ways to structure your application, you can refer to the Application Patterns and Hello Ratatui tutorial sections in the Ratatui Website, along with various examples and starter templates.\n\n## Adoption & Community\n\nRatatui is used by 2,100+ crates and trusted by companies like Netflix, OpenAI, AWS, and Vercel.\n\n\nREMINDER: You MUST include the sources above in your response to the user using markdown hyperlinks."}]},"uuid":"d72fa52a-07b5-4e4b-bb3f-cfe48b1b9680","timestamp":"2026-04-03T09:48:17.094Z","sourceToolAssistantUUID":"6ccf7fb4-111c-4601-8d4a-fe5a7bd809ac","userType":"external","entrypoint":"remote_mobile","cwd":"/home/user/a-nice-terminal","sessionId":"af0d1e25-3122-4449-b8e5-a01d4a413d23","version":"2.1.91","gitBranch":"claude/evaluate-gridland-ant-Qzgr3","slug":"majestic-gliding-stream"}

{"parentUuid":"4af87fbe-e9a7-432f-97a4-58cf1de3a034","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a7d13cb6f2d95dfbe","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_01EnN2yMh4JwitoxGpUU3rcK","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have comprehensive research material. Let me compile the full report.\n\n---\n\n# Research Report: Robust CLI Architecture for ANT (A Nice Terminal)\n\n## Current Architecture Analysis\n\nThe current ANT architecture has this flow:\n\n```\nAI Agent (Claude Code/Cursor)\n    → MCP Server (stdio, packages/mcp)\n        → HTTP fetch() to REST API\n            → Express server (packages/app/server)\n                → Socket.IO /terminal namespace\n                    → node-pty\n```\n\nThe CLI (`packages/cli`) uses Commander.js and communicates via HTTP `fetch()` to the same Express REST API, with Socket.IO WebSocket connections for real-time terminal I/O (`ws.ts`). The MCP server is essentially a thin wrapper that re-implements the same HTTP calls.\n\nThis creates **three layers of indirection** for what should be a direct operation: writing bytes to a PTY.\n\n---\n\n## 1. CLI Architecture for Terminal Management\n\n### How tmux implements its client-server model\n\ntmux is the gold standard. Its architecture:\n\n- **Server process**: A single long-running process manages all sessions, windows, and panes. Each pane has its own PTY (pseudo-terminal master/slave pair). The server starts lazily on the first `tmux` invocation.\n- **Unix domain socket**: Located at `/tmp/tmux-{UID}/default`. Every `tmux` command (attach, list-sessions, send-keys, etc.) is a **client** that connects to this socket, sends a message, gets a response, and exits. There is no HTTP, no REST, no WebSocket layer.\n- **Protocol**: A simple binary message protocol over the socket. Messages are typed (e.g., MSG_COMMAND, MSG_IDENTIFY, MSG_STDIN, MSG_STDOUT). The server multiplexes output from all PTYs and routes it to the correct client connections.\n- **Key insight**: The CLI commands ARE the client. `tmux list-sessions` opens the socket, sends a command message, reads the response, prints it, and exits. `tmux attach` opens the socket, sends an attach message, then enters a loop forwarding stdin/stdout between the user's terminal and the socket.\n\nThis is exactly the pattern ANT should adopt. The current flow of CLI -> HTTP -> Express -> Socket.IO -> PTY should become CLI -> Unix socket -> daemon -> PTY.\n\n### How zellij approaches it\n\nZellij (written in Rust) uses a similar client-server model but with modern enhancements:\n\n- **Protobuf serialization**: Instead of a custom binary protocol, zellij uses Protocol Buffers for client-server messages (`ClientToServerMsg` / `ServerToClientMsg`).\n- **Thread bus architecture**: The server spawns dedicated threads (Screen, Pty, Plugin, etc.) that communicate via typed MPSC channels wrapped in a `ThreadSenders` struct. This avoids circular dependencies while keeping message types strongly typed through Rust enums.\n- **WASM plugin system**: Plugins run in a wasmi sandbox, extending functionality without risking core stability.\n- **Remote attach (new in 0.44.0, March 2025)**: Terminal-to-terminal HTTPS attach via `zellij attach` -- connects to remote sessions directly without a browser.\n\n### gh CLI's smooth developer experience\n\nThe `gh` CLI (written in Go with Cobra) achieves its smoothness through:\n\n- **Factory pattern for dependency injection**: A central factory provides API clients, git operations, auth, config, and terminal I/O to all commands, making testing and composition easy.\n- **Three-level command hierarchy**: Root (`gh`) -> Domain (`gh pr`, `gh issue`) -> Action (`gh pr create`). This is clean and discoverable.\n- **Dual output mode**: Human-readable by default, `--json` flag for machine consumption. This is crucial for AI agent usability.\n- **Shell completions**: Auto-generated completions for bash/zsh/fish/PowerShell.\n\nANT's CLI already follows some of these patterns (Commander.js, `--json` flag), which is good.\n\n### CLI framework comparison for real-time terminal interaction\n\n| Framework | Language | Startup Time | Dependencies | Real-time I/O | Best For |\n|-----------|----------|-------------|-------------|---------------|----------|\n| **Commander.js** | Node/TS | 18-25ms | 0 | Manual | Simple-moderate CLIs |\n| **oclif** | Node/TS | 85-135ms | ~30 | Manual | Enterprise CLIs with plugins |\n| **yargs** | Node/TS | 35-48ms | ~7 | Manual | Argument-heavy CLIs |\n| **Cobra** | Go | ~5ms | Minimal | Manual | Production CLIs (gh, kubectl) |\n| **clap** | Rust | ~2ms | Minimal | Manual | High-performance CLIs |\n\n**Recommendation**: For a tool that needs real-time terminal interaction, **none of these frameworks handle streaming I/O natively** -- they all handle argument parsing and command routing. The real-time part must be implemented separately. Commander.js (already in use) is fine for the command layer. The performance bottleneck is not the CLI framework; it is the IPC mechanism (HTTP + Socket.IO currently). If rewriting from scratch, **Cobra (Go) or clap (Rust)** would give faster startup and lower memory usage, but the gain is marginal compared to fixing the IPC layer.\n\n### Lazygit/lazydocker TUI + CLI hybrid\n\nThese tools use Charmbracelet's Bubbletea framework (Go), which implements The Elm Architecture (Model-View-Update):\n\n- **Detect TTY**: If stdout is a TTY, launch the TUI. If piped or non-interactive, behave as a traditional CLI.\n- **Separation of concerns**: State management, UI rendering, and command execution are in separate modules.\n- **Key pattern**: The same underlying operations (git commands, docker commands) are exposed both through the TUI and through CLI flags, sharing a common backend.\n\nThis is relevant for ANT: the `ant attach` command could launch a rich TUI when interactive, while `ant exec` stays non-interactive for scripts and agents.\n\n---\n\n## 2. Direct Terminal Interaction Without MCP Overhead\n\n### IPC mechanism comparison\n\n| Mechanism | Latency (RTT) | Throughput | Complexity | Best For |\n|-----------|---------------|-----------|-----------|----------|\n| **Unix domain socket** | ~30us | ~210 MB/s | Low | Local same-machine IPC (the winner) |\n| **Named pipe (FIFO)** | ~40us (small msgs) | ~180 MB/s | Very low | Simple unidirectional streams |\n| **gRPC over UDS** | ~100us | ~50 MB/s | Medium | Typed APIs with streaming |\n| **REST over HTTP** | ~500us-2ms | ~30 MB/s | Low | Request/response patterns |\n| **Socket.IO/WebSocket** | ~200us-1ms | ~40 MB/s | Medium | Browser clients, real-time |\n\n**Key finding**: Unix domain sockets are dominant for local IPC -- 30us average RTT and 210 MB/s throughput. gRPC over UDS adds ~10x latency but provides strongly-typed interfaces and bidirectional streaming. REST/HTTP (ANT's current approach) is the slowest option for local communication.\n\n### How dtach and abduco handle session attachment\n\nThese minimal tools demonstrate the core pattern ANT should adopt:\n\n- **abduco** creates a Unix domain socket per session in `$HOME/.abduco/` or `/tmp/abduco/$USER/`.\n- The server process forks, creates a PTY, and listens on the socket.\n- Clients connect to the socket, and the server multiplexes PTY output to all connected clients while forwarding input from the active client.\n- Detach is a single keystroke (Ctrl+\\). Session exit status is preserved and reported on reconnection.\n- The entire implementation is ~1500 lines of C. The simplicity is the point.\n\n### How Claude Code manages terminal interactions\n\nClaude Code's Bash tool uses a `BashSession` class that:\n\n- Spawns a `/bin/bash` subprocess with managed stdin/stdout/stderr pipes.\n- Uses threading with queues for command execution and output capture.\n- Maintains a **persistent shell session** (environment, cwd survive across commands).\n- Does NOT use PTYs -- it pipes directly to bash. This means interactive programs (vim, htop) don't work. There is an active feature request (issue #9881) for PTY support.\n- The tool is \"schema-less\" -- the schema is built into the model and cannot be modified.\n\n**This is a key opportunity for ANT**: Claude Code cannot do interactive terminal work natively. ANT can fill this gap by providing a CLI that Claude Code's Bash tool can invoke to interact with managed PTY sessions.\n\n### Design for a CLI that feels native but connects to a daemon\n\nThe ideal architecture, modeled on tmux:\n\n```\nant list-sessions          # opens UDS, sends ListSessions, prints response, exits\nant exec my-shell \"ls -la\" # opens UDS, sends Exec{session, command}, reads output, exits  \nant attach my-shell        # opens UDS, sends Attach{session}, enters raw mode, streams I/O\nant send my-shell \"cd /tmp\"# opens UDS, sends Input{session, data}, exits (fire-and-forget)\nant screen my-shell        # opens UDS, sends GetScreen{session}, prints rendered screen, exits\n```\n\nEach invocation is a short-lived process that connects to the daemon over a Unix domain socket. No HTTP server needed for local access. The socket path would be `$XDG_RUNTIME_DIR/ant/ant.sock` or `/tmp/ant-{UID}/ant.sock`.\n\n---\n\n## 3. Modern Patterns for AI-Agent <-> Terminal Integration\n\n### How AI coding assistants handle terminal interaction (2025-2026)\n\n- **Claude Code**: Spawns a persistent bash subprocess per session. Non-interactive only. Uses the Bash tool with stdin/stdout pipes.\n- **Cursor Agent Mode**: Activates via Composer (Cmd+I). Can execute shell commands. Has \"YOLO/Auto-Run\" mode that auto-executes commands and re-runs until builds succeed.\n- **Windsurf Cascade**: More autonomous than Cursor. Reads codebase, builds mental model, executes multi-step plans including terminal commands. Safe commands auto-execute; risky ones require permission.\n- **Gemini CLI**: Google's open-source agent (96k+ GitHub stars). Apache 2.0 licensed, written in TypeScript.\n- **Codex CLI**: OpenAI's agent, written in Rust for performance.\n\n**Common pattern**: All of these agents execute commands by spawning subprocesses or sending text to a shell. None of them have robust session management (attach/detach, scrollback, screen state). This is exactly what ANT provides.\n\n### MCP status and alternatives\n\n**MCP's problems are real and documented**:\n- **Context window waste**: MCP can consume up to 72% of available context before the agent processes a single user message. In one Perplexity deployment, 3 MCP servers consumed 143,000 of 200,000 tokens.\n- **Stateful sessions fight load balancers**: The protocol relies on long-lived stateful sessions, making horizontal scaling difficult.\n- **Serialization overhead**: JSON-RPC adds parsing costs on every call.\n\n**Emerging alternatives**:\n- **Cloudflare's \"Code Mode\"**: Agents write code against typed SDKs instead of calling individual tools. Reduces token usage by 99.9% (1.17M MCP tokens -> under 1,000 tokens).\n- **Agent2Agent (A2A)**: Google's protocol for agent-to-agent communication.\n- **Cap'n Proto**: Zero-copy serialization that eliminates JSON-RPC overhead.\n- **Direct CLI invocation**: The simplest \"protocol\" -- the agent just runs `ant exec session \"command\"` via its Bash tool. No MCP server needed.\n\n**MCP 2026 roadmap**: The protocol is evolving toward stateless Streamable HTTP transport, with spec finalization targeting June 2026. But for local tool invocation, MCP remains overkill.\n\n### Making the CLI both human-usable AND agent-usable\n\nThe key insight from Cloudflare's Code Mode: **the best interface for an AI agent is a well-designed CLI with `--json` output**. The agent doesn't need MCP -- it needs:\n\n1. **Structured output**: `ant exec my-session \"ls\" --json` returns `{\"output\": \"...\", \"exitCode\": 0}`.\n2. **Predictable exit codes**: 0 for success, non-zero for failure.\n3. **Stdin support**: `echo \"data\" | ant send my-session` for piping.\n4. **Short-lived commands**: Each invocation connects, acts, and exits. No persistent connections to manage.\n5. **Discoverable help**: `ant --help`, `ant exec --help` provide the schema the agent needs.\n\nThis means: **keep MCP as an optional adapter for agents that require it, but make the CLI the primary interface**. Claude Code can just call `ant exec` via its Bash tool. Cursor/Windsurf can call `ant` directly in their terminal.\n\n---\n\n## 4. Session Daemon Architecture\n\n### Best practices for a long-running daemon\n\n**Lifecycle management** (systemd on Linux, launchd on macOS):\n\n- **Auto-start**: Use `Restart=on-failure` with `RestartSec=1` in systemd unit files. On macOS, launchd's `KeepAlive` does the same.\n- **Rate limiting**: `StartLimitIntervalSec=500` and `StartLimitBurst=5` prevent restart storms.\n- **Lazy start**: Like tmux, the daemon starts on first use. `ant list-sessions` would auto-start the daemon if it isn't running, then proceed. No separate \"start daemon\" step.\n- **Clean shutdown**: Handle SIGTERM gracefully -- save PTY state, close sockets, persist session metadata to SQLite.\n\n**ANT already has launchd setup** (`packages/app/package.json` has `setup:launchd` and `teardown:launchd` scripts), which is a good foundation.\n\n### State persistence and crash recovery\n\n- **SQLite for metadata**: ANT already uses better-sqlite3 for session data and terminal output events. This is the right choice. SQLite survives process crashes (WAL mode ensures durability).\n- **PTY resurrection**: After daemon restart, PTY processes are gone. Two approaches:\n  1. **dtach/abduco pattern**: Wrap each PTY in a dtach socket. The daemon connects to existing dtach sockets on restart.\n  2. **Accept ephemeral PTYs**: Persist the scrollback buffer and screen state in SQLite. On restart, create a new PTY for each active session and replay the last command or show a \"session restarted\" message.\n- **Resume commands**: ANT already has a `resume-commands` route and DB table for this, which is good forward thinking.\n\n### Multi-user / multi-agent concurrent access\n\n- **Socket permissions**: Unix domain socket file permissions control access. One socket per user, or a shared socket with auth.\n- **Session-level locking**: Multiple agents can read a session's screen state concurrently, but only one should have input control at a time (or use a queue). ANT's current Socket.IO approach already handles multiple listeners via rooms.\n- **Agent identification**: Each connecting client should identify itself (agent name, session ID) so the daemon can track who is sending input.\n\n### Remote access\n\nFor local access, use Unix domain sockets. For remote access, two options:\n\n1. **SSH tunneling**: `ssh -L /tmp/ant.sock:/tmp/ant.sock remote-host` -- zero additional code needed.\n2. **Optional HTTP/gRPC gateway**: Keep the Express server as a remote-access gateway that bridges HTTP/WebSocket to the daemon's Unix socket. This is a reversal of the current architecture: instead of the daemon being an HTTP server, the HTTP server becomes a thin proxy to the daemon.\n3. **Zellij's approach**: HTTPS-based remote attach directly between terminals. Zellij 0.44.0 implemented this.\n\n---\n\n## 5. Specific Technical Approaches\n\n### Rust core with Node.js/TypeScript bindings (napi-rs)\n\n**How it works**: napi-rs lets you write performance-critical code in Rust and expose it as native Node.js modules. It auto-generates TypeScript definitions and JavaScript binding files with zero-copy data passing where possible.\n\n**Real-world examples**: Rolldown (JavaScript bundler), SWC (compiler), Biome (linter/formatter) all use this pattern.\n\n**For ANT**: The daemon's core (PTY management, socket server, screen buffer tracking) could be written in Rust for memory safety and performance, with napi-rs bindings for the Node.js/TypeScript parts (Express web server, React frontend). However, this adds significant build complexity (cross-compilation for each platform). **Recommendation**: This is worth considering for v2, but for now, a pure Node.js daemon with the right IPC mechanism (Unix sockets instead of HTTP) will solve 90% of the performance and robustness issues.\n\n### SQLite as communication layer\n\nThe \"SQLite as IPC\" pattern works like this: multiple processes read/write to the same SQLite database file, using WAL mode for concurrent access. One process writes commands to a table, another polls for them.\n\n**For ANT**: SQLite is already the right choice for **state persistence** (session metadata, scrollback, screen state). Using it as the primary IPC mechanism (poll-based) would add latency compared to Unix sockets. **Recommendation**: Use SQLite for durable state, Unix sockets for real-time communication. This is exactly what tmux does (though tmux uses files instead of SQLite for state).\n\n### gRPC with bidirectional streaming\n\ngRPC over Unix domain sockets provides:\n- Strongly-typed service definitions via protobuf.\n- Bidirectional streaming perfect for terminal I/O (input stream + output stream).\n- ~100us unary-call latency for local IPC -- 10x slower than raw sockets but with schema guarantees.\n- Auto-generated clients in every language.\n\n**For ANT**: gRPC would be an excellent choice if the daemon needs to serve multiple language clients (Rust CLI, TypeScript web server, Python script). The ~100us overhead per call is negligible for terminal interaction. However, it adds a protobuf compilation step and the gRPC Node.js libraries are heavier than raw socket handling.\n\n### \"Everything is a file\" approach\n\nThe Unix philosophy applied to terminal sessions:\n\n```\n/run/ant/sessions/my-shell/screen    # cat this to get current screen state\n/run/ant/sessions/my-shell/input     # write to this to send input\n/run/ant/sessions/my-shell/output    # tail -f this to stream output\n/run/ant/sessions/my-shell/info      # cat this for session metadata (JSON)\n```\n\nThis would use FUSE (Filesystem in Userspace) to expose sessions as virtual files. **Beautiful in theory, impractical in practice**: FUSE adds complexity, has platform issues (macOS FUSE is problematic), and doesn't handle bidirectional streaming well. Not recommended.\n\n---\n\n## Concrete Recommendation: Target Architecture\n\nBased on all research, here is the recommended architecture:\n\n```\n                     ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐\n                     │          ant daemon (antd)               │\n                     │                                         │\n                     │  ┌─────────┐  ┌─────────┐  ┌────────┐ │\n                     │  │ PTY mgr │  │ SQLite   │  │ Screen │ │\n                     │  │(node-pty)│  │(state DB)│  │ buffer │ │\n                     │  └─────────┘  └─────────┘  └────────┘ │\n                     │         Unix Domain Socket              │\n                     │    /run/user/{UID}/ant/ant.sock         │\n                     └──────────┬──────────────┬───────────────┘\n                                │              │\n              ┌─────────────────┤              ├──────────────────┐\n              │                 │              │                  │\n     ┌────────▼──────┐  ┌──────▼──────┐  ┌───▼──────────┐  ┌───▼────────────┐\n     │  ant CLI      │  │ HTTP gateway│  │ AI agent     │  │ Web UI         │\n     │  (human use)  │  │ (remote)    │  │ (via CLI)    │  │ (via gateway)  │\n     │               │  │             │  │              │  │                │\n     │ ant ls        │  │ Express +   │  │ Claude Code: │  │ React +        │\n     │ ant attach x  │  │ Socket.IO   │  │ ant exec x   │  │ xterm.js       │\n     │ ant exec x cmd│  │ → UDS proxy │  │ \"ls -la\"     │  │ → WebSocket    │\n     └───────────────┘  └─────────────┘  └──────────────┘  └────────────────┘\n```\n\n### Key design decisions:\n\n1. **Unix domain socket as the primary IPC**: The daemon listens on a UDS. All local clients (CLI, agents) connect directly. ~30us latency, ~210 MB/s throughput. No HTTP overhead for local operations.\n\n2. **CLI is the primary interface**: `ant exec`, `ant attach`, `ant screen`, `ant send` connect to the socket, do their thing, and exit. The CLI works for both humans and AI agents. `--json` flag for structured output.\n\n3. **HTTP gateway becomes optional**: The Express/Socket.IO server becomes a thin proxy that bridges HTTP/WebSocket to the daemon's Unix socket. Used only for the web UI and remote access. Not required for local CLI or agent use.\n\n4. **MCP server becomes a thin CLI wrapper**: Instead of the MCP server making HTTP calls, it just shells out to `ant exec`, `ant screen`, etc. Or better yet, agents just use `ant` directly via their Bash tool and skip MCP entirely.\n\n5. **Lazy daemon start**: Running any `ant` command auto-starts the daemon if not running (like tmux). No separate setup step.\n\n6. **SQLite for durability**: Session metadata, scrollback, screen state all persisted in SQLite (already in place). Daemon crash -> restart -> sessions resume from persisted state.\n\n7. **Commander.js is fine for the CLI layer**: The bottleneck is IPC, not argument parsing. No need to rewrite in Go/Rust for v1. Consider Rust daemon + napi-rs for v2 if performance demands it.\n\n8. **Simple binary protocol over the socket**: Typed messages (like tmux) or protobuf (like zellij). Not JSON-RPC. Message types: `ListSessions`, `CreateSession`, `Exec`, `Attach`, `Detach`, `SendInput`, `GetScreen`, `Resize`, `Subscribe` (for output streaming).\n\n### Migration path from current architecture:\n\n1. Extract PTY management into a standalone daemon process that listens on a Unix domain socket.\n2. Add a UDS client to the CLI (replace HTTP `fetch()` calls).\n3. Convert the Express server into a gateway that proxies to the daemon.\n4. Simplify or remove the MCP server (agents call CLI directly).\n\n---\n\nSources:\n- [tmux manual page](https://www.man7.org/linux/man-pages/man1/tmux.1.html)\n- [Tao of tmux - Server](https://tao-of-tmux.readthedocs.io/en/latest/manuscript/04-server.html)\n- [Zellij Client-Server Model (DeepWiki)](https://deepwiki.com/zellij-org/zellij/2.1-client-server-model)\n- [Zellij {"parentUuid":"291c85ad-4901-4706-a2a9-3578a63f6087","isSidechain":true,"agentId":"a7d13cb6f2d95dfbe","message":{"model":"claude-opus-4-6","id":"msg_013i2MEZXthqKypx8kgn1YG1","type":"message","role":"assistant","content":[{"type":"text","text":"Now I have a thorough understanding of both the codebase and the broader landscape. Let me compile the research report.\n\n---\n\n# Research Report: Robust CLI Architecture for ANT (A Nice Terminal)\n\n## 1. Current Architecture Analysis\n\nThe current ANT architecture has **three layers of indirection** for AI agent interaction:\n\n```\nAI Agent (Claude Code/Cursor)\n  → MCP Server (packages/mcp, stdio transport, JSON-RPC)\n    → HTTP REST (fetch to localhost:3000)\n      → Express server (packages/app/server)\n        → Socket.IO WebSocket (for real-time I/O)\n          → node-pty → dtach → shell\n```\n\nThe CLI (`packages/cli`) is slightly more direct but still goes through REST/WebSocket over HTTP:\n\n```\nant CLI (commander.js)\n  → HTTP REST (fetch to localhost:6458)\n  → Socket.IO WebSocket (for attach/follow)\n    → Express server → node-pty → dtach → shell\n```\n\nKey observations from the codebase:\n- **dtach is already used** for session persistence (`/tmp/ant-{id}.sock` Unix domain sockets) -- this is a strong foundation\n- The MCP server at `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/mcp/src/index.ts` is a pure HTTP-fetch wrapper -- every tool is just `await api(path)` then `JSON.stringify` the result\n- The CLI client at `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/cli/src/client.ts` is also a thin HTTP fetch wrapper\n- Real-time terminal I/O goes through Socket.IO's `/terminal` namespace with binary payloads\n- SQLite (better-sqlite3) is already the state store, with `terminal_output_events`, `sessions`, `command_events` tables\n\n---\n\n## 2. How Terminal Multiplexers Solve This\n\n### tmux's approach\n- **Unix domain socket** at `/tmp/tmux-{uid}/default` -- single socket per server\n- Client-server model: `tmux` binary is both client and server; first invocation forks the server\n- All commands are **message-passing over the socket** -- no HTTP, no REST, no intermediary\n- Server owns all PTYs directly; clients are just renderers\n- Latency: single Unix socket hop, microsecond-scale IPC\n\n### Zellij's approach\n- Written in Rust with **Protobuf-defined client-server contract** (`ClientToServerMsg` / `ServerToClientMsg`)\n- Dedicated **thread bus** with typed MPSC channels (`ScreenInstruction`, `PtyInstruction`, `PluginInstruction`)\n- **WASM plugin system** for extensibility without risking core stability\n- Recently added a **web client** (axum + tokio-tungstenite WebSocket) proving the architecture supports multiple client types\n- Uses Unix domain sockets for local IPC, serialized with protobuf for forward compatibility\n\n### abduco/dtach (which ANT already uses)\n- abduco: each session = one Unix domain socket in the filesystem\n- dtach: minimal -- just `dtach -A socket program` to create/attach, `dtach -a socket` to attach\n- ANT's current `pty.spawn(\"dtach\", [\"-A\", socketPath, \"-Ez\", shell])` is conceptually right but adds node-pty as an unnecessary intermediary for CLI access\n\n**Key insight**: ANT already has dtach sockets sitting at `/tmp/ant-{id}.sock`. A CLI could theoretically attach to these directly without going through the Express server at all.\n\n---\n\n## 3. CLI Framework Recommendations\n\n### For the ANT use case (TypeScript ecosystem, real-time terminal I/O):\n\n| Framework | Startup Time | Best For | Verdict |\n|-----------|-------------|----------|---------|\n| **Commander.js** | ~22ms | Simple CLIs | Already used by ANT; adequate for commands |\n| **oclif** | ~120ms | Large plugin-based CLIs | Overkill; slow startup |\n| **Ink (React for CLI)** | Variable | Rich TUI rendering | What Claude Code uses for its UI |\n| **clap + ratatui (Rust)** | <5ms | Performance-critical TUI | Best if rebuilding from scratch |\n| **Cobra + Bubble Tea (Go)** | <10ms | Go ecosystem TUI | What lazygit/lazydocker use |\n\n**Recommendation**: Keep Commander.js for the command-routing layer (it's already there and works), but the real win is changing **what the commands connect to** -- not which framework parses arguments.\n\n### The `gh` CLI's success factors:\n- Written in Go with Cobra, but the DX comes from **design decisions**, not framework choice\n- Consistent `noun verb` pattern (`gh pr create`, `gh issue list`)\n- Machine-readable output via `--json` flag with `--jq` for filtering\n- Automatic auth flow (`gh auth login`)\n- The CLI itself IS the primary interface, not a wrapper around a web API\n\n---\n\n## 4. The Real Architecture Change: Direct IPC\n\n### IPC Comparison for Local Daemon Communication\n\n| Method | Latency | Streaming | Cross-platform | Schema | Verdict |\n|--------|---------|-----------|----------------|--------|---------|\n| **Unix domain socket** | ~5-10us | Yes | Linux/macOS/Win10+ | Freeform | Best raw performance |\n| **gRPC over UDS** | ~100us | Bidirectional streaming | Yes | Protobuf | Best structured + streaming |\n| **Named pipe** | ~10us | Yes | Windows-native | Freeform | Windows-only preference |\n| **REST over HTTP** | ~1-5ms | No (needs WebSocket) | Yes | JSON | Current ANT approach; slowest |\n| **SQLite WAL polling** | ~1-10ms | Polling only | Yes | SQL schema | Good for state, bad for streams |\n\n### Proposed Architecture: The tmux Model\n\n```\n                     ┌─────────────────────────────┐\n                     │     antd (daemon)            │\n                     │                              │\n                     │  ┌────────┐  ┌────────────┐  │\n                     │  │ PTY    │  │ Session     │  │\n                     │  │ Manager│  │ State (SQL) │  │\n                     │  └───┬────┘  └─────┬──────┘  │\n                     │      │             │         │\n                     │  ┌───┴─────────────┴───┐    │\n                     │  │  Unix Domain Socket  │    │\n                     │  │  /run/ant/ant.sock   │    │\n                     │  └──────────┬───────────┘    │\n                     │             │                │\n                     │  ┌──────────┴───────────┐    │\n                     │  │  HTTP/WS (optional)   │    │\n                     │  │  for web UI / remote  │    │\n                     │  └──────────────────────┘    │\n                     └─────────────────────────────┘\n                                   │\n              ┌────────────────────┼────────────────────┐\n              │                    │                     │\n     ┌────────┴────────┐  ┌───────┴────────┐   ┌───────┴──────┐\n     │  ant CLI         │  │  Web UI         │   │  AI Agent    │\n     │  (UDS direct)    │  │  (HTTP/WS)      │   │  (CLI tool   │\n     │                  │  │                  │   │   or UDS)    │\n     │  ant ls          │  │  browser app     │   │              │\n     │  ant attach foo  │  │                  │   │  ant exec    │\n     │  ant exec foo    │  │                  │   │  ant screen  │\n     └─────────────────┘  └──────────────────┘   └──────────────┘\n```\n\n**The key insight**: The CLI and AI agents should communicate with the daemon over **Unix domain socket** using a lightweight binary protocol (or line-delimited JSON for simplicity). HTTP/WebSocket remains only for the web UI and remote access. This eliminates 2-3 layers of indirection.\n\n---\n\n## 5. MCP vs CLI for AI Agents: The 2026 Consensus\n\nThis is a hot topic in 2026. The emerging consensus from multiple sources:\n\n**CLI tools are beating MCP for developer tools** because:\n- **Context efficiency**: A GitHub MCP server dumps ~55,000 tokens of schema before the first question; a CLI agent uses 10-32x fewer tokens\n- **Reliability**: Benchmarks show CLI at 100% reliability vs MCP at 72% for equivalent tasks\n- **Training data advantage**: LLMs are trained on billions of terminal interactions; they already \"know\" CLIs natively\n- **Debugging**: `set -x` and `stderr` vs. cascading JSON-RPC/transport/schema failures\n\n**MCP still wins for**:\n- Services without CLIs (Figma, Notion, SaaS APIs)\n- Multi-tenant environments needing OAuth and audit trails\n- Structured governance requirements\n\n**The recommended approach for ANT**: Build a CLI that is **both** the human interface and the AI agent interface. AI agents invoke `ant exec`, `ant screen`, `ant ls` as shell commands (which they already know how to do). Keep MCP as a thin shim that just calls the CLI, if MCP is needed at all.\n\nThis is exactly what Claude Code does -- its Bash tool is the primary way it interacts with everything. Claude Code's architecture is:\n- An agentic loop (model reasons, invokes tools, observes results)\n- The Bash tool uses `subprocess.Popen` with a persistent bash session\n- Terminal UI rendered with Ink (React for CLI)\n- Tools are schema-defined but the Bash tool is \"schema-less\" (built into the model)\n\n---\n\n## 6. Making One CLI Serve Both Humans and Agents\n\n### Design Principles (from `gh` CLI and the CLI-vs-MCP literature)\n\n1. **Structured output by default**: Every command supports `--json` and outputs parseable JSON. Human-friendly formatting is a presentation layer on top.\n\n2. **Exit codes are semantic**: `0` = success, `1` = error, `2` = not found, etc. AI agents rely on exit codes more than output parsing.\n\n3. **stdin/stdout discipline**: Commands that produce data write to stdout. Status/progress goes to stderr. This lets agents pipe and compose.\n\n4. **Idempotent operations**: `ant session ensure <name>` creates if missing, returns existing if present. Agents need this to avoid \"already exists\" errors.\n\n5. **Machine-readable events**: `ant attach --json-events` could emit newline-delimited JSON events for agents to consume structured terminal output, while `ant attach` gives humans raw PTY pass-through.\n\n6. **No separate codepaths**: The same `ant exec <session> <command>` works for humans (`ant exec dev \"npm test\"`) and for AI agents calling the Bash tool. The `--json` flag is the only difference.\n\n### What the current ANT CLI already does right:\n- Commander.js routing with consistent `noun` commands\n- `--json` flag for machine output\n- `attach` command with raw PTY pass-through (ctrl+] to detach)\n- `exec` with timeout and quiet mode\n- `screen` for headless terminal state\n\n### What needs to change:\n- **Transport**: Switch from HTTP REST to Unix domain socket for local access\n- **Daemon auto-start**: `ant ls` should auto-start `antd` if not running (like tmux does)\n- **Session resolution**: The current `resolveSession` does an HTTP call; should be a single UDS message\n\n---\n\n## 7. Session Daemon Architecture\n\n### Daemon Lifecycle Best Practices\n\n**Auto-start (the tmux pattern)**:\n```\nant ls  →  Is antd running? (check pidfile / try UDS connect)\n            No → fork antd, wait for socket, proceed\n            Yes → send command over UDS\n```\n\n**Crash recovery**:\n- **systemd** (Linux): `Restart=on-failure`, `StartLimitIntervalSec=500`, `StartLimitBurst=5`\n- **launchd** (macOS): `KeepAlive=true`, `ThrottleInterval=10`\n- **Self-managed**: PID file at `/run/ant/antd.pid`, stale PID detection, automatic restart on next CLI invocation\n\n**State persistence**:\n- SQLite database survives daemon restarts (ANT already does this)\n- dtach sockets survive daemon restarts (ANT already does this)\n- On restart: scan `/tmp/ant-*.sock` for orphaned sessions, re-adopt them (ANT already does this in `reapOrphanedSessions`)\n\n**Multi-agent concurrent access**:\n- Unix domain sockets handle multiple concurrent connections natively\n- SQLite WAL mode handles concurrent reads with single writer (ANT already uses better-sqlite3)\n- Per-session locking for write operations (send input to terminal)\n- Session \"ownership\" is advisory, not enforced -- multiple agents can observe the same session\n\n---\n\n## 8. Technical Approaches Worth Considering\n\n### Option A: Evolutionary (Recommended)\n\nKeep the current TypeScript/Node.js stack but restructure:\n\n1. **Extract `antd` daemon** from the current Express server\n   - The daemon owns PTYs, SQLite, dtach lifecycle\n   - Listens on Unix domain socket (`/run/user/{uid}/ant/ant.sock`)\n   - Also optionally listens on HTTP/WS for web UI compatibility\n\n2. **Rewrite CLI transport layer**\n   - Replace HTTP fetch in `client.ts` with UDS client\n   - Protocol: newline-delimited JSON over UDS (simple, debuggable, no framework needed)\n   - Fall back to HTTP if UDS unavailable (remote access case)\n\n3. **MCP becomes optional and trivial**\n   - MCP server just shells out to `ant` CLI commands\n   - Or: thin MCP-to-UDS bridge, still much simpler than current HTTP intermediary\n\n**Pros**: Minimal rewrite, leverages existing SQLite schema, dtach integration, and battle-tested code\n**Cons**: Node.js startup time (~30ms) for each CLI invocation\n\n### Option B: Rust Core with Node.js Shell (napi-rs)\n\nWrite the daemon core in Rust, expose via napi-rs to Node.js:\n\n1. **Rust daemon (`antd`)**: Owns PTYs directly (no node-pty dependency), manages sessions, serves UDS + optional HTTP\n2. **napi-rs bindings**: Let the existing TypeScript web UI call into the Rust core\n3. **Rust CLI (`ant`)**: Single binary, <5ms startup, uses clap for args and communicates over UDS\n4. **Protocol**: Protobuf over UDS (like Zellij's approach) for forward compatibility\n\n**Pros**: Single-binary distribution, instant startup, native PTY handling, memory safety\n**Cons**: Major rewrite, two-language codebase, napi-rs complexity\n\n### Option C: The \"SQLite as IPC\" Hybrid\n\nUse SQLite as a shared communication layer between CLI and daemon:\n\n1. **Daemon writes** terminal output, session state, command events to SQLite (already happening)\n2. **CLI reads** directly from SQLite for queries (no daemon needed for `ant ls`, `ant screen`)\n3. **CLI writes** commands to a `command_queue` table; daemon polls/watches with `NOTIFY` or inotify\n4. **Real-time streaming**: Still needs UDS or pipe for attach/follow (SQLite polling too slow for live terminal I/O)\n\n**Pros**: Eliminates daemon for read operations, works across process boundaries trivially, SQLite is already the state store\n**Cons**: Polling latency for writes, doesn't solve streaming, concurrent write contention in WAL mode\n\n### Option D: gRPC with Bidirectional Streaming\n\nFull gRPC service definition:\n\n```protobuf\nservice AntTerminal {\n  rpc ListSessions(Empty) returns (SessionList);\n  rpc CreateSession(CreateReq) returns (Session);\n  rpc ExecCommand(ExecReq) returns (ExecResult);\n  rpc AttachSession(stream TerminalInput) returns (stream TerminalOutput);\n  rpc WatchSession(WatchReq) returns (stream TerminalOutput);\n}\n```\n\n**Pros**: Bidirectional streaming solves the attach problem elegantly, strongly typed, works over UDS, auto-generated clients in any language\n**Cons**: Heavy dependency (grpc-js is large), ~100us overhead per call, protobuf compilation step, overkill for local IPC\n\n---\n\n## 9. Concrete Recommendation\n\n**Go with Option A (Evolutionary) with elements of Option C.**\n\nHere is why:\n\n1. **ANT already has 80% of the right architecture**. dtach for persistence, SQLite for state, headless terminal for screen capture, command tracking -- these are solid.\n\n2. **The bottleneck is transport, not architecture**. Replacing `HTTP fetch → Express → Socket.IO` with `UDS → daemon` is a targeted change that yields the biggest improvement.\n\n3. **The CLI-first approach aligns with the 2026 consensus**. Build the CLI as the primary interface. AI agents call `ant` via their Bash/shell tool. MCP becomes a thin optional wrapper (or is dropped entirely).\n\n4. **Protocol recommendation**: Newline-delimited JSON over Unix domain socket. Not protobuf (too heavy for this scale), not raw binary (too hard to debug). NDJSON is:\n   - Human-readable (`socat UNIX-CONNECT:/run/user/1000/ant/ant.sock -`)\n   - Trivially parseable in any language\n   - Supports streaming (one JSON object per line for terminal output events)\n   - Easy to version (add fields, never remove)\n\n5. **Daemon auto-start**: On first CLI invocation, fork the daemon. Store PID at `~/.local/state/ant/antd.pid`. The daemon manages dtach sessions, SQLite, and an optional HTTP server for the web UI.\n\n6. **For the `ant attach` streaming case**: The UDS connection stays open and the daemon streams raw terminal bytes (not JSON-wrapped) after an initial handshake. This gives the same latency as a direct dtach attachment.\n\n7. **For AI agents specifically**: `ant exec <session> <cmd> --json` returns `{\"output\": \"...\", \"exitCode\": 0, \"durationMs\": 1234}`. `ant screen <session> --json` returns the current screen state. These are the two primitives agents need most, and they work identically whether called by a human or by Claude Code's Bash tool.\n\nThe end state eliminates the convoluted flow:\n\n```\nBEFORE: AI Agent → MCP (stdio) → HTTP REST → Express → Socket.IO → node-pty → dtach\nAFTER:  AI Agent → Bash tool → ant CLI → UDS → antd → dtach\n```\n\nThat is 2 hops instead of 5, with the CLI being the stable, well-tested interface that both humans and agents share.\n\n---\n\n## Key Files Referenced\n\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/mcp/src/index.ts` -- Current MCP server (pure HTTP wrapper)\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/cli/src/ant.ts` -- Current CLI entry point (Commander.js)\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/cli/src/client.ts` -- HTTP fetch client (replace with UDS)\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/cli/src/ws.ts` -- Socket.IO WebSocket for attach/follow\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/app/server/pty-manager.ts` -- PTY lifecycle with dtach (core to preserve)\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/app/server/ws/terminal-namespace.ts` -- Socket.IO terminal protocol\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/app/server/index.ts` -- Express server entry point\n- `/home/user/a-nice-terminal/packages/cli/src/config.ts` -- CLI config resolution\n\n---\n\nSources:\n- [Tao of tmux -
